			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Belief</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>

				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">This article is about the general concept. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Belief_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Belief (disambiguation)">Belief (disambiguation)</a>.</div>

<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Certainty_sidebar" title="Template:Certainty sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>

<p><b>Belief</b> is the state of <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a> in which a person thinks something to be the case, with or without there being empirical evidence to prove that something is the case with factual certainty. Another way of defining belief sees it as a <a href="/wiki/Mental_representation" title="Mental representation">mental representation</a> of an <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">attitude positively oriented</a> towards the <a href="/wiki/Likelihood" class="mw-redirect" title="Likelihood">likelihood</a> of something being <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">true</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> In the context of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek thought</a>, two related concepts were identified<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2016)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> with regards to the concept of belief: <i><a href="/wiki/Pistis#Pistis_in_rhetoric" title="Pistis">pistis</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i>. Simplified, we may say that <i>pistis</i> refers to "trust" and "confidence", while <i>doxa</i> refers to "opinion" and "acceptance". The English word "orthodoxy" derives from <i>doxa</i>. Jonathan Leicester suggests that belief has the purpose of guiding action rather than indicating truth.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>In epistemology, philosophers use the term "belief" to refer to personal attitudes associated with true or false ideas and concepts. However, "belief" does not require active introspection and circumspection. For example, we never ponder whether or not the sun will rise. We simply assume the sun will rise. Since "belief" is an important aspect of mundane life, according to Eric Schwitzgebel in the <i>Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</i>, a related question asks: "how a physical organism can have beliefs?"<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:238px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Belief_Venn_diagram.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Belief_Venn_diagram.svg/236px-Belief_Venn_diagram.svg.png" width="236" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Belief_Venn_diagram.svg/354px-Belief_Venn_diagram.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Belief_Venn_diagram.svg/472px-Belief_Venn_diagram.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="658" data-file-height="420" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Belief_Venn_diagram.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn</a>/<a href="/wiki/Euler_diagram" title="Euler diagram">Euler diagram</a> which grants that <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a> and belief may be distinguished and that their <a href="/wiki/Intersection_(set_theory)" title="Intersection (set theory)">intersection</a> is <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>. Unsurprisingly, this is <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">a controversial analysis</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>.</div>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Knowledge_and_epistemology">Knowledge and epistemology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Knowledge and epistemology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">Epistemology</a> is concerned with delineating the boundary between justified belief and <a href="/wiki/Opinion" title="Opinion">opinion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> and involved generally with a theoretical philosophical study of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a>. The primary problem in epistemology is to understand exactly what is needed in order for us to have knowledge. In a notion derived from <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus</a></i>, where the epistemology of Socrates (Platon) most clearly departs from that of the <a href="/wiki/Sophist" title="Sophist">sophists</a>, who at the time of Plato seem to have defined knowledge as what is here expressed as "<a href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>". The tendency to translate from belief (here: doxa - common opinion) to knowledge (here: episteme), which Plato (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> of the dialogue) utterly dismisses, results from failing to distinguish a <a href="/wiki/Dispositive" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispositive">dispositive</a> belief (gr. 'doxa', not 'pistis') from knowledge (episteme) when the opinion is regarded <i>true</i> (here: orth√©), in terms of right, and juristically so (according to the premises of the dialogue). Which was the task of the <a href="/wiki/Rhetor" class="mw-redirect" title="Rhetor">rhetors</a> to prove. Plato dismisses this possibility of an affirmative relation between belief (i.e. opinion) and knowledge even when the one who opines grounds his belief on the rule, and is able to add <i>justification</i> (gr. logos: reasonable and necessarily plausible assertions/evidence/guidance) to it <a rel="nofollow" class="external autonumber" href="http://www.friesian.com/knowledg.htm">[3]</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It is important to keep in mind that the sort of belief in the context of <i>Theaetetus</i> is not derived from the theological concept of belief, which is <i>pistis</i>, but <i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i>, which in theological terms refers to acceptance in the form of praise and glory.</p>
<p>Strangely, or not, Plato has been credited for the "<i>justified true belief</i>" theory of knowledge, even though Plato in the <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theaetetus (dialogue)</a> elegantly dismisses it, and even posits this argument of Socrates as a cause for his death penalty . Among American epistemologists, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gettier" title="Edmund Gettier">Gettier</a> (1963)<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Goldman" title="Alvin Goldman">Goldman</a> (1967),<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> have questioned the "justified true belief" definition, and challenged the "sophists" of their time.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="As_a_psychological_phenomenon">As a psychological phenomenon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: As a psychological phenomenon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Mainstream psychology and related disciplines have traditionally treated belief as if it were the simplest form of mental representation and therefore one of the building blocks of conscious thought. Philosophers have tended to be more abstract in their analysis, and much of the work examining the viability of the belief concept stems from philosophical analysis.</p>
<p>The concept of belief presumes a subject (the believer) and an object of belief (the proposition). So, like other <a href="/wiki/Propositional_attitude" title="Propositional attitude">propositional attitudes</a>, belief implies the existence of <a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mental_state" class="extiw" title="wikt:mental state">mental states</a> and <a href="/wiki/Intentionality" title="Intentionality">intentionality</a>, both of which are hotly debated topics in the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mind" title="Philosophy of mind">philosophy of mind</a>, whose foundations and relation to brain states are still controversial.</p>
<p>Beliefs are sometimes divided into <a href="/wiki/Core_beliefs" class="mw-redirect" title="Core beliefs">core beliefs</a> (that are actively thought about) and <i><a href="/wiki/Dispositional_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Dispositional belief">dispositional beliefs</a></i> (that may be ascribed to someone who has not thought about the issue). For example, if asked "do you believe tigers wear pink pajamas?" a person might answer that they do not, despite the fact they may never have thought about this situation before.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>This has important implications for understanding the <a href="/wiki/Neuropsychology" title="Neuropsychology">neuropsychology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> of belief. If the concept of belief is incoherent, then any attempt to find the underlying neural processes that support it will fail.</p>
<p>Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Rudder_Baker" title="Lynne Rudder Baker">Lynne Rudder Baker</a> has outlined four main contemporary approaches to belief in her controversial book <i>Saving Belief</i>:<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is correct - Sometimes called the "mental sentence theory," in this conception, beliefs exist as coherent entities, and the way we talk about them in everyday life is a valid basis for scientific endeavour. <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Fodor" title="Jerry Fodor">Jerry Fodor</a> is one of the principal defenders of this point of view.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief may not be entirely correct, but it is close enough to make some useful predictions - This view argues that we will eventually reject the idea of belief as we use it now, but that there may be a correlation between what we take to be a belief when someone says "I believe that snow is white" and how a future theory of psychology will explain this behaviour. Most notably, philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Stich" title="Stephen Stich">Stephen Stich</a> has argued for this particular understanding of belief.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is entirely wrong and will be completely superseded by a radically different theory that will have no use for the concept of belief as we know it - Known as <a href="/wiki/Eliminativism" class="mw-redirect" title="Eliminativism">eliminativism</a>, this view (most notably proposed by <a href="/wiki/Paul_Churchland" title="Paul Churchland">Paul</a> and <a href="/wiki/Patricia_Churchland" title="Patricia Churchland">Patricia Churchland</a>) argues that the concept of belief is like obsolete theories of times past such as <a href="/wiki/The_four_humours" class="mw-redirect" title="The four humours">the four humours</a> theory of medicine, or the <a href="/wiki/Phlogiston_theory" title="Phlogiston theory">phlogiston theory</a> of combustion. In these cases science hasn't provided us with a more detailed account of these theories, but completely rejected them as valid scientific concepts to be replaced by entirely different accounts. The Churchlands argue that our common-sense concept of belief is similar in that as we discover more about neuroscience and the brain, the inevitable conclusion will be to reject the belief hypothesis in its entirety.</li>
<li>Our common-sense understanding of belief is entirely wrong; however, treating people, animals, and even computers as if they had beliefs is often a successful strategy - The major proponents of this view, <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Dennett" title="Daniel Dennett">Daniel Dennett</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lynne_Rudder_Baker" title="Lynne Rudder Baker">Lynne Rudder Baker</a>, are both <a href="/wiki/Eliminativism" class="mw-redirect" title="Eliminativism">eliminativists</a> in that they hold that beliefs are not a scientifically valid concept, but they don't go as far as rejecting the concept of belief as a predictive device. Dennett gives the example of playing a computer at chess. While few people would agree that the computer held beliefs, treating the computer as if it did (e.g. that the computer believes that taking the opposition's queen will give it a considerable advantage) is likely to be a successful and predictive strategy. In this understanding of belief, named by Dennett <i>the <a href="/wiki/Intentional_stance" title="Intentional stance">intentional stance</a></i>, belief-based explanations of mind and behaviour are at a different level of explanation and are not reducible to those based on fundamental neuroscience, although both may be explanatory at their own level.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemological_belief_compared_to_religious_belief">Epistemological belief compared to religious belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Epistemological belief compared to religious belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Historically <i>belief-in</i> belonged in the realm of religious thought, <i>belief-that</i> instead belonged to epistemological considerations.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief-in">Belief-in</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Belief-in">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>To "believe in" someone or something is a distinct concept from "believing-that." There are at least these types of belief-in:<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li><b>Commendatory</b> / <b><a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">Faith</a></b> - we may make an expression of 'faith' in respect of some performance by an agent X, when without prejudice to the truth value of the factual outcome or even confidence in X otherwise, we expect that specific performance. In particular <a href="/wiki/Self-confidence" title="Self-confidence">self-confidence</a> or faith in one's self is this kind of belief.</li>
<li><b>Existential claim</b> - to claim belief in the existence of an entity or phenomenon in a general way with the implied need to justify its claim to existence. It is often used when the entity is not real, or its existence is in doubt. "He believes in witches and ghosts" or "many children believe in Santa Claus" or "I believe in a deity" are typical examples.<sup id="cite_ref-belief-in_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-belief-in-12">[12]</a></sup> The linguistic form is distinct from the assertion of the truth of a proposition since verification is either considered impossible/irrelevant or a <a href="/wiki/Counterfactual" class="mw-redirect" title="Counterfactual">counterfactual</a> situation is assumed.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Belief-that">Belief-that</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Belief-that">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Economical_belief">Economical belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Economical belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Economic beliefs are beliefs which are reasonably and necessarily contrary to the tenet of rational choice or instrumental rationality.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies of the Austrian tradition of the economic thought, in the context of analysis of the influence and subsequent degree of change resulting from existing economic knowledge and belief, has contributed the most to the subsequent holistic collective analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-R._Arena_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-R._Arena-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Delusion">Delusion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Delusion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Insofar as the truth of belief is expressed in sentential and propositional form we are using the sense of <i>belief-that</i> rather than <i>belief-in</i>. <a href="/wiki/Delusion" title="Delusion">Delusion</a> arises when the truth value of the form is clearly nil.<sup id="cite_ref-L._Bortolotti_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-L._Bortolotti-15">[15]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>Delusions are defined as beliefs in <a href="/wiki/Psychiatric" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychiatric">psychiatric</a> diagnostic criteria<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> (for example in the <i><a href="/wiki/Diagnostic_and_Statistical_Manual_of_Mental_Disorders" title="Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders">Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders</a></i>). Psychiatrist and historian <a href="/wiki/G.E._Berrios" class="mw-redirect" title="G.E. Berrios">G.E. Berrios</a> has challenged the view that delusions are genuine beliefs and instead labels them as "empty speech acts," where affected persons are motivated to express false or bizarre belief statements due to an underlying psychological disturbance. However, the majority of mental health professionals and researchers treat delusions as if they were genuine beliefs.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Carroll" title="Lewis Carroll">Lewis Carroll</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Through_the_Looking-Glass" title="Through the Looking-Glass">Through the Looking-Glass</a></i> the <a href="/wiki/White_Queen_(Through_the_Looking-Glass)" title="White Queen (Through the Looking-Glass)">White Queen</a> says, "Why, sometimes I've believed as many as six impossible things before breakfast." This is often quoted in mockery of the common ability of people to <a href="/wiki/Credo_quia_absurdum" title="Credo quia absurdum">entertain beliefs contrary to fact</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Formation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ripples_of_influence.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/220px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/330px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Ripples_of_influence.jpg/440px-Ripples_of_influence.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3888" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ripples_of_influence.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
We are influenced by many factors that ripple through our minds as our beliefs form, evolve, and may eventually change</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Psychologists study belief formation and the relationship between beliefs and actions. Three models of belief formation and change have been proposed:</p>
<dl>
<dt>The Conditional Inference Process</dt>
</dl>
<p>When people are asked to estimate the likelihood that a statement is true, they search their memory for information that has implications for the validity of this statement. Once this information has been identified, they estimate a) the likelihood that the statement would be true if the information were true, and b) the likelihood that the statement would be true if the information were false. If their estimates for these two probabilities differ, people average them, weighting each by the likelihood that the information is true and false (respectively). Thus, information bears directly on beliefs of another, related statement.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer.2C_R._S._2005_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer.2C_R._S._2005-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Linear Models of Belief Formation</dt>
</dl>
<p>Unlike the previous model, this one takes into consideration the possibility of multiple factors influencing belief formation. Using regression procedures, this model predicts belief formation on the basis of several different pieces of information, with weights assigned to each piece on the basis of their relative importance.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer.2C_R._S._2005_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer.2C_R._S._2005-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<dl>
<dt>Information Processing Models of Belief Formation and Change</dt>
</dl>
<p>These models address the fact that the responses people have to belief-relevant information is unlikely to be predicted from the objective basis of the information that they can recall at the time their beliefs are reported. Instead, these responses reflect the number and meaning of the thoughts that people have about the message at the time that they encounter it.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer.2C_R._S._2005_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer.2C_R._S._2005-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some influences on people's belief formation include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Internalization of beliefs during childhood, which can form and shape our beliefs in different domains. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> is often quoted as having said that "Common sense is the collection of prejudices acquired by age eighteen." Political beliefs depend most strongly on the political beliefs most common in the community where we live.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Most individuals believe the <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> they were taught in childhood.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></li>
<li>Charismatic leaders can form and/or modify beliefs (even if those beliefs fly in the face of all previous beliefs).<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Is belief voluntary? Rational individuals need to reconcile their direct reality with any said belief; therefore, if belief is not present or possible, it reflects the fact that contradictions were necessarily overcome using <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_dissonance" title="Cognitive dissonance">cognitive dissonance</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Advertising" title="Advertising">Advertising</a> can form or change beliefs through repetition, shock, and association with images of sex, love, beauty, and other strong positive emotions.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Contrary to intuition, a delay, known as the <a href="/wiki/Sleeper_effect" title="Sleeper effect">sleeper effect</a>, instead of immediate succession may increase an advertisement's ability to persuade viewer's beliefs if a discounting cue is present.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></li>
<li>Physical trauma, especially to the head, can radically alter a person's beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>However, even educated people, well aware of the process by which beliefs form, still strongly cling to their beliefs, and act on those beliefs even against their own self-interest. In Anna Rowley's book, Leadership Therapy, she states "You want your beliefs to change. It's proof that you are keeping your eyes open, living fully, and welcoming everything that the world and people around you can teach you." This means that peoples' beliefs should evolve as they gain new experiences.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Justified_true_belief">Justified true belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Justified true belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Justified true belief is a definition of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> that gained approval during the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a>, 'justified' standing in contrast to 'revealed'. There have been attempts to trace it back to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a> and his dialogues.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The concept of justified true belief states that in order to know that a given proposition is true, one must not only believe the relevant true proposition, but also have justification for doing so. In more formal terms, an agent <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >
  <semantics>
    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

      </mstyle>
    </mrow>
    <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle S}</annotation>
  </semantics>
</math></span><img src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" aria-hidden="true" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.51ex; height:2.176ex;" alt="S" /></span> knows that a proposition <span><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML" >

<ol>

  <semantics>

</ol>
<p>This theory of knowledge suffered a significant setback with the discovery of <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">Gettier problems</a>, situations in which the above conditions were seemingly met but that many philosophers disagree that anything is known.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Nozick" title="Robert Nozick">Robert Nozick</a> suggested a <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problems#Fred_Dretske.27s_conclusive_reasons_and_Robert_Nozick.27s_truth-tracking" class="mw-redirect" title="Gettier problems">clarification</a> of "justification" which he believed eliminates the problem: the justification has to be such that were the justification false, the knowledge would be false. If so we can say belief becomes knowledge (accepted reality) when it is justified. Many philosophers has since attempted to eliminate the Gettier problems by adding a fourth condition, changing the conditions, or arguing that the agent still has knowledge in the Gettier cases.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modification">Modification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Modification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Belief_revision" title="Belief revision">Belief revision</a></div>
<p>An extensive amount of scientific research and philosophical discussion exists around the modification of beliefs, which is commonly referred to as belief revision. Generally speaking, the process of belief revision entails the believer weighing the set of truths and/or evidence, and the dominance of a set of truths or evidence on an alternative to a held belief can lead to revision. One process of belief revision is <a href="/wiki/Bayesian_inference" title="Bayesian inference">Bayesian updating</a> and is often referenced for its mathematical basis and conceptual simplicity. However, such a process may not be representative for individuals whose beliefs are not easily characterized as probabilistic.</p>
<p>There are several techniques for individuals or groups to change the beliefs of others; these methods generally fall under the umbrella of <a href="/wiki/Persuasion" title="Persuasion">persuasion</a>. Persuasion can take on more specific forms such as <a href="/wiki/Consciousness_raising" title="Consciousness raising">consciousness raising</a> when considered in an activist or political context. Belief modification may also occur as a result of the experience of outcomes. Because <a href="/wiki/Goal" title="Goal">goals</a> are based, in part on beliefs, the success or failure at a particular goal may contribute to modification of beliefs that supported the original goal.</p>
<p>Whether or not belief modification actually occurs is dependent not only on the extent of truths or evidence for the alternative belief, but also characteristics outside the specific truths or evidence. This includes, but is not limited to: the source characteristics of the message, such as <a href="/wiki/Source_credibility" title="Source credibility">credibility</a>; <a href="/wiki/Peer_pressure" title="Peer pressure">social pressures</a>; the anticipated consequences of a modification; or the ability of the individual or group to act on the modification. Therefore, individuals seeking to achieve belief modification in themselves or others need to consider all possible forms of resistance to belief revision.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Partial">Partial</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Partial">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Without qualification, "belief" normally implies a lack of <a href="/wiki/Doubt" title="Doubt">doubt</a>, especially insofar as it is a designation of a <a href="/wiki/Life_stance" title="Life stance">life stance</a>. In practical everyday use however, belief is normally partial and retractable with varying degrees of certainty.</p>
<p>A copious literature exists in multiple disciplines to accommodate this reality. In mathematics <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probability</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_logic" title="Fuzzy logic">fuzzy logic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fuzzy_set_theory" class="mw-redirect" title="Fuzzy set theory">fuzzy set theory</a>, and other topics are largely directed to this.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prediction">Prediction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Prediction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Different psychological models have tried to predict people's beliefs and some of them try to estimate the exact probabilities of beliefs. For example, <a href="/wiki/Robert_S._Wyer" title="Robert S. Wyer">Robert Wyer</a> developed a model of subjective probabilities.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer.2C_R._S._1970_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer.2C_R._S._1970-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WyerGoldberg_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WyerGoldberg-30">[30]</a></sup> When people rate the likelihood of a certain statement (e.g., "It will rain tomorrow"), this rating can be seen as a subjective probability value. The subjective probability model posits that these subjective probabilities follow the same rules as objective probabilities. For example, the <a href="/wiki/Law_of_total_probability" title="Law of total probability">law of total probability</a> might be applied to predict a subjective probability value. Wyer found that this model produces relatively accurate predictions for probabilities of single events and for changes in these probabilities, but that the probabilities of several beliefs linked by "and" or "or" do not follow the model as well.<sup id="cite_ref-Wyer.2C_R._S._1970_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wyer.2C_R._S._1970-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WyerGoldberg_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WyerGoldberg-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Religion_collage_updated.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Religion collage updated.jpg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/220px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/330px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Religion_collage_updated.jpg/440px-Religion_collage_updated.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2017" data-file-height="1382" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Religion_collage_updated.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Religious belief refers to attitudes towards <a href="/wiki/Mythological" class="mw-redirect" title="Mythological">mythological</a>, <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spiritual</a> aspects of a <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a>. Religious belief is <a href="/wiki/Distinct" title="Distinct">distinct</a> from <a href="/wiki/Religious_ritual" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious ritual">religious practice</a> or <a href="/wiki/Religious_behaviour" title="Religious behaviour">religious behaviours</a> with some believers not practicing <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> and some practitioners not believing religion. Religious beliefs, being derived from ideas that are exclusive to religion, often relate to the existence, characteristics and worship of a <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">deity</a> or deities, <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">divine intervention</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and human life, or the <a href="/wiki/Deontological" class="mw-redirect" title="Deontological">deontological</a> explanations for the values and practices centered on the teachings of a spiritual leader or group. In contrast to other <a href="/wiki/Belief_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Belief system">belief systems</a>, religious beliefs are usually <a href="/wiki/Codification_(law)" title="Codification (law)">codified</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_of_religious_belief">Forms of religious belief</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Forms of religious belief">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While it is popularly conceived that religions each have identifiable and exclusive sets of beliefs or <a href="/wiki/Creed" title="Creed">creeds</a>, surveys of religious belief have often found that the official doctrine and descriptions of the beliefs offered by <a href="/wiki/Religious_authority" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious authority">religious authorities</a> do not always agree with the privately held beliefs of those who identify as members of a particular religion.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> A broad classification of the kinds of religious belief is documented below</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Fundamentalism">Fundamentalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Fundamentalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_fundamentalism" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious fundamentalism">Religious fundamentalism</a></div>
<p>First self-applied as a term to the conservative doctrine outlined by anti-modernist <a href="/wiki/Protestants" class="mw-redirect" title="Protestants">Protestants</a> in the United States of America,<sup id="cite_ref-A.C._Dixon_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-A.C._Dixon-33">[33]</a></sup> fundamentalism as a religious belief is associated with a strict adherence to an interpretation of scriptures that are generally associated with theologically conservative positions or traditional understandings of the text and are distrustful of innovative readings, new revelation, or alternate interpretations. Religious fundamentalism has been identified in the media as being associated with <a href="/wiki/Fanaticism" title="Fanaticism">fanatical</a> or <a href="/wiki/Zealotry" class="mw-redirect" title="Zealotry">zealous</a> political movements around the world that have used a strict adherence to a particular religious doctrine as a means to establish political identity and enforce societal norms.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthodoxy">Orthodoxy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Orthodoxy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Orthodoxy_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Orthodoxy (disambiguation)">Orthodoxy (disambiguation)</a></div>
<p>First used in the context of <a href="/wiki/Early_Christianity" title="Early Christianity">Early Christianity</a>, orthodoxy is a religious belief that closely follows the edicts, apologies, and hermeneutics of a prevailing religious authority. In the case of Early Christianity, this authority was the communion of bishops, and is often referred to by the term <a href="/wiki/Magisterium" title="Magisterium">Magisterium</a>. The term <i>orthodox</i> was applied almost as an epithet to a group of Jewish believers who held to pre-Enlightenment understanding of Judaism and now known as <a href="/wiki/Orthodox_Judaism" title="Orthodox Judaism">Orthodox Judaism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Church</a> of Christianity, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>, consider themselves to be the true heir to the Early Christian belief and practice. The antonym of orthodox is <a href="/wiki/Heterodox" class="mw-redirect" title="Heterodox">heterodox</a> and those adhering to orthodoxy often accuse the heterodox of <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">apostasy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schism_(religion)" class="mw-redirect" title="Schism (religion)">schism</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modernism.2Freform">Modernism/reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Modernism/reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Enlightenment" title="Age of Enlightenment">Enlightenment</a> in Europe were associated with varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a> and intolerance towards new religious ideas. The <a href="/wiki/Philosophe" class="mw-redirect" title="Philosophe">Philosophes</a> took particular exception to many of the more fantastical claims of religions and directly challenged religious authority and the prevailing beliefs associated with the established churches. In response to the liberalizing political and social movements, some religious groups attempted to integrate Enlightenment ideals of rationality, equality, and individual liberty into their belief systems, especially into the nineteenth and twentieth centuries. <a href="/wiki/Reform_Judaism" title="Reform Judaism">Reform Judaism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Christianity" title="Liberal Christianity">Liberal Christianity</a> are two examples of such religious associations.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Superstition">Superstition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Superstition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Superstition" title="Superstition">Superstition</a></div>
<p>A term signifying derogation that is used by the religious and non-religious alike, superstition is the deprecated belief in supernatural causation. Those who deny the existence of the supernatural generally attribute all beliefs associated with it to be superstitious while a typical religious critique of superstition holds that it either encompasses beliefs in non-existent supernatural activity or that the supernatural activity is inappropriately feared or held in improper regard (see <a href="/wiki/Idolatry" title="Idolatry">idolatry</a>). <a href="/wiki/Occultism" class="mw-redirect" title="Occultism">Occultism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Animism" title="Animism">animism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Folk_religion" title="Folk religion">folk religions</a> were strongly condemned by Christian Churches as mean forms of superstition, though such condemnation did not necessarily eliminate the beliefs among the common people and many such religious beliefs persist to today.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Systemization">Systemization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Systemization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In Buddhism, practice and progress along the spiritual path happens when one follows the system of <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_practice" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist practice">Buddhist practice</a>. Any religion which follows (parts of) the fundamentals of this system has, according to the teachings of Buddha, good aspects to the extent it accords with this system. Any religion which goes against (parts of) the fundamentals of this system, includes bad aspects too. Any religion which does not teach certain parts of this system, is not because of this a 'bad' religion; it just lacks those teachings and is to that extent incomplete.</p>
<p>A question by the monk Subhadda to the Buddha:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"O Gotama, there are <a href="/wiki/Shramana" class="mw-redirect" title="Shramana">Samanas</a> (wandering monks) and <a href="/wiki/Brahmin" title="Brahmin">Brahmanas</a> (religious leaders) who are leaders of their sects, who are well-esteemed by many people, such as Purana Kassapa, Makkhali Gosala, Ajita Kesakambala, Pakudha Kaccayana, Sancaya Belatthaputta and <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Nigantha Nataputta</a>. Do all of them have knowledge and understanding as they themselves have declared? Or do all of them have no knowledge and understanding?"</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The reply by Buddha was:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>"Subhadda, in whatever teaching is not found the <a href="/wiki/Noble_Eightfold_Path" title="Noble Eightfold Path">Noble Eightfold Path</a>, neither in it is there found a Samana of the <a href="/wiki/Four_stages_of_enlightenment#Stream-enterer" title="Four stages of enlightenment">first stage</a>, nor a Samana of the <a href="/wiki/Four_stages_of_enlightenment#Once-returner" title="Four stages of enlightenment">second stage</a>, nor a Samana of the <a href="/wiki/Four_stages_of_enlightenment#Non-returner" title="Four stages of enlightenment">third stage</a>, nor a Samana of the <a href="/wiki/Four_stages_of_enlightenment#Arahant" title="Four stages of enlightenment">fourth stage</a>."</p>
</blockquote>
<p>As a religious tradition, <a href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hinduism</a> has experienced many attempts at systemization. In medieval times, <a href="/wiki/Adi_Shankara" title="Adi Shankara">Shankara</a> advocated for the <a href="/wiki/Advaita" class="mw-redirect" title="Advaita">Advaita</a> system of philosophy. In recent times, <a href="/wiki/Tamala_Krishna_Gosvami" class="mw-redirect" title="Tamala Krishna Gosvami">Tamala Krishna Gosvami</a> has researched the systemization of <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> theology as expounded by <a href="/wiki/Srila_Prabhupada" class="mw-redirect" title="Srila Prabhupada">Srila Prabhupada</a>. (See <a href="/wiki/Krishnology" class="mw-redirect" title="Krishnology">Krishnology</a>)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Universalism">Universalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Universalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some believe that religion cannot be separated from other aspects of life, or believe that certain cultures did not or do not separate their religious activities from other activities in the same way that some people in modern <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western</a> cultures do.</p>
<p>Some <a href="/wiki/Anthropologist" title="Anthropologist">anthropologists</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (July 2010)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> report cultures in which gods are involved in every aspect of life - if a cow goes dry, a god has caused this, and must be propitiated, when the sun rises in the morning, a god has caused this, and must be thanked. Even in modern Western cultures, many people see supernatural forces behind every event, as described by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">Carl Sagan</a> in his book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Demon-Haunted_World" title="The Demon-Haunted World">The Demon-Haunted World</a></i>.</p>
<p>People with this worldview often consider the influence of Western culture to be inimical. Others with this world view resist the influence of <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, and believe that science, or "so-called science", should be guided by religion. Still others with this worldview believe that all political decisions and laws should be guided by religion. This last belief is written into the constitution of many <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islamic</a> nations, and is shared by some <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist_Christians" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamentalist Christians">fundamentalist Christians</a>.</p>
<p>In addition, beliefs about the <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">supernatural</a> or <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> may not presuppose a difference between any such thing as <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> and non-nature, nor between science and what the most educated people believe. In the view of some historians<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (September 2013)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup>, the pre-<a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socratic</a> <a href="/wiki/Athenian" class="mw-redirect" title="Athenian">Athenians</a> saw <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, political <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a>, <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> and religion as not easily distinguishable, but all part of the same body of <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a> available to a <a href="/wiki/Community" title="Community">community</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Approaches_to_the_beliefs_of_others">Approaches to the beliefs of others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Approaches to the beliefs of others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Adherents of particular religions deal with the differing doctrines and practices espoused by other religions in a variety of ways. All strains of thought appear in different segments of all major world religions.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Exclusivism">Exclusivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Exclusivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Exclusivism" title="Exclusivism">Exclusivism</a></div>
<p>People with exclusivist beliefs typically explain other religions as either in error, or as corruptions or counterfeits of the true faith. This approach is a fairly consistent feature among smaller <a href="/wiki/New_religious_movement" title="New religious movement">new religious movements</a> that often rely on doctrine that claims a unique <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">revelation</a> by the founder or leaders, and consider it a matter of faith that the religion has a monopoly on truth. All three major Abrahamic monotheistic religions have passages in their holy scriptures that attest to the primacy of the scriptural testimony and indeed monotheism itself is often couched as an innovation characterized specifically by its explicit rejection of earlier polytheistic faiths.</p>
<p>Some exclusivist faiths incorporate a specific element of <a href="/wiki/Proselytization" class="mw-redirect" title="Proselytization">proselytization</a>. This is a strongly held belief in the Christian tradition which follows the doctrine of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Commission" title="Great Commission">Great Commission</a>, and is less emphasized by the Islamic faith where the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quranic</a> edict "There shall be no compulsion in religion" (2:256) is often quoted as a justification for toleration of alternative beliefs, while the <a href="/wiki/Conversion_to_Judaism" title="Conversion to Judaism">Jewish tradition</a> is one that does not actively seek out converts.</p>
<p>Exclusivism correlates with conservative, fundamentalist, and orthodox approaches of many religions while pluralistic and syncretist approaches either explicitly downplay or reject the exclusivist tendencies of the religion.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Inclusivism">Inclusivism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Inclusivism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>People with <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">inclusivist</a> beliefs recognize some truth in all faith systems, highlighting agreements and minimizing differences. The attitude is sometimes associated with <a href="/wiki/Interfaith_dialog" class="mw-redirect" title="Interfaith dialog">Interfaith dialogue</a> or the Christian <a href="/wiki/Ecumenical" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecumenical">Ecumenical</a> movement, though in principle such attempts at pluralism are not necessarily inclusivist and many actors in such interactions (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" class="mw-redirect" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a>) still hold to exclusivist dogma while participating in inter-religious organizations.</p>
<p>Explicitly inclusivist religions include many that are associated with the <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">New Age</a> movement as well as modern reinterpretations of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah√°'√≠ Faith">Bah√°'√≠ Faith</a> considers it doctrine that there is truth in all faith systems.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluralism">Pluralism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pluralism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">Religious pluralism</a></div>
<p>People with <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">pluralist</a> beliefs make no distinction between faith systems, viewing each one as valid within a particular culture. Examples include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Extracts from the Sri <a href="/wiki/Guru_Granth_Sahib" title="Guru Granth Sahib">Guru Granth Sahib</a> Ji (Sikh Holy Scriptures), "There is only the One Supreme Lord God; there is no other at all" (Pannaa 45). "By His Power the <a href="/wiki/Vedas" title="Vedas">Vedas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Purana" class="mw-redirect" title="Purana">Puranas</a> exist, and the Holy Scriptures of the Jewish, Christian and Islamic religions. By His Power all deliberations exist." (Pannaa 464). "Some call Him, '<a href="/wiki/Lord_Rama" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Rama">Ram</a>, Ram', and some call Him, 'Khudaa-i'. Some serve Him as 'Gusain', others as 'Allaah'. ||1|| He is the Cause of causes, the Generous Lord. He showers His Grace and Mercy upon us amen." (Pannaa 885).</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Syncretism">Syncretism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Syncretism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">Syncretism</a></div>
<p>People with syncretistic views blend the views of a variety of different religions or traditional beliefs into a unique fusion which suits their particular experience and context (<i>see</i> <a href="/wiki/Eclecticism" title="Eclecticism">eclecticism</a>). <a href="/wiki/Unitarian_Universalism" title="Unitarian Universalism">Unitarian Universalism</a> is an example of a syncretistic faith.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adherence">Adherence</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Adherence">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God</a></div>
<p>Typical reasons for adherence to religion include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Belief in God is seen by some to be necessary for <a href="/wiki/Morality" title="Morality">moral behavior</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-moore_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moore-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
<li>Many people consider religious practices to be serene, beautiful, and conducive to religious experiences, which in turn support religious beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note--35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>Organized religions promote a sense of community among their followers, and the moral and cultural common ground of these communities makes them attractive to people with the same values.<sup id="cite_ref-repa_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-repa-36">[36]</a></sup> Indeed, while religious beliefs and practices are usually connected, some individuals with substantially secular beliefs still participate in religious practices for cultural reasons.</li>
<li>Each religion asserts that it is a means by which its adherents may come into closer contact with God, Truth, and Spiritual Power. They all promise to free adherents from spiritual bondage, and bring them into spiritual freedom. It naturally follows that a religion which frees its adherents from deception, sin, and spiritual death will have significant mental health benefits. <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Maslow" title="Abraham Maslow">Abraham Maslow</a>'s research after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> showed that <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">Holocaust</a> survivors tended to be those who held strong religious beliefs (not necessarily temple attendance, etc.), suggesting it helped people cope in extreme circumstances. <a href="/wiki/Humanistic_psychology" title="Humanistic psychology">Humanistic psychology</a> went on to investigate how religious or spiritual identity may have correlations with longer lifespan and better health. The study found that humans may particularly need religious ideas to serve various emotional needs such as the need to feel loved, the need to belong to homogeneous groups, the need for understandable explanations and the need for a guarantee of ultimate justice. Other factors may involve sense of purpose, sense of identity, sense of contact with the divine. See also <i>Man's Search for Meaning</i>, by Victor Frankl, detailing his experience with the importance of religion in surviving the Holocaust. Critics assert that the very fact that religion was the primary selector for research subjects may have introduced a bias, and that the fact that all subjects were holocaust survivors may also have had an effect. According to Larson et al. (2000), "[m]ore longitudinal research with better multidimensional measures will help further clarify the roles of these [religious] factors and whether they are beneficial or harmful."<sup id="cite_ref-larson_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-larson-37">[37]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Apostasy">Apostasy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Apostasy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Apostasy" title="Apostasy">Apostasy</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God#Arguments_against_belief_in_God" title="Existence of God">Existence of God ¬ß&#160;Arguments against belief in God</a></div>
<p>Typical reasons for rejection of religion include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The fundamental doctrines of some religions are considered by some to be illogical, contrary to experience, or unsupported by sufficient evidence, and are rejected for those reasons.<sup id="cite_ref-russell_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-russell-38">[38]</a></sup> Even some believers may have difficulty accepting particular religious assertions or doctrines. Some people believe the body of evidence available to humans to be insufficient to justify certain religious beliefs. They may thus disagree with religious interpretations of ethics and human purpose, or various <a href="/wiki/Creation_myth" title="Creation myth">creation myths</a>. This reason has perhaps been aggravated by the protestations of some <a href="/wiki/Fundamentalist_Christianity" class="mw-redirect" title="Fundamentalist Christianity">fundamentalist Christians</a>.</li>
<li>Some religions include beliefs that certain groups of people are inferior or sinful and deserve contempt, persecution, or even death, and that non-believers will be punished for their unbelief in an after-life.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></li>
<li>Some people may be unable to accept the values that a specific religion promotes and will therefore not join that religion. They may also be unable to accept the proposition that those who do not believe will go to hell or be damned, especially if said nonbelievers are close to the person.</li>
<li>The maintenance of life and the achievement of self-esteem require of a person the fullest exercise of reason‚Äîbut morality, people are taught, rests on and requires faith.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Systems">Systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A <i>belief system</i> is a set of mutually supportive beliefs. The beliefs of any such system can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Religious_belief" class="mw-redirect" title="Religious belief">religious</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophical</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideological</a>, or a combination of these. Philosopher <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Glover" title="Jonathan Glover">Jonathan Glover</a> says that beliefs are always part of a belief system, and that tenanted belief systems are difficult for the tenants to completely revise or reject.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gilbert.2C_sociological_perspectives">Gilbert, sociological perspectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Gilbert, sociological perspectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A collective belief is referred to when people speak of what 'we' believe when this is not simply elliptical for what 'we all' believe.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">Sociologist</a> <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="√âmile Durkheim">√âmile Durkheim</a> wrote of collective beliefs and proposed that they, like all '<a href="/wiki/Social_fact" title="Social fact">social facts</a>', 'inhered in' social <a href="/wiki/Group_(sociology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Group (sociology)">groups</a> as opposed to individual persons. Durkheim's discussion of collective belief, though suggestive, is relatively obscure.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">Philosopher</a> <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Gilbert" title="Margaret Gilbert">Margaret Gilbert</a> has offered a related account in terms of the joint commitment of a number of persons to accept a certain belief as a body. According to this account, individuals who together collectively believe something need not personally believe it themselves. Gilbert's work on the topic has stimulated a developing literature among philosophers. One question that has arisen is whether and how philosophical accounts of belief in general need to be sensitive to the possibility of collective belief.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Glover">Glover</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Glover">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Jonathan Glover believes that he and other philosophers ought to play some role in starting dialogues between people with deeply held, opposing beliefs, especially if there is risk of violence. Glover also believes that philosophy can offer insights about beliefs that would be relevant to such dialogue.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Snipa.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/240px-Snipa.JPG" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/360px-Snipa.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/Snipa.JPG/480px-Snipa.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="2112" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Snipa.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Philosopher Jonathan Glover warns that belief systems are like whole boats in the water; it is extremely difficult to alter them all at once (e.g., it may be too stressful, or people may maintain their biases without realizing it).<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Glover suggests that beliefs have to be considered holistically, and that no belief exists in isolation in the mind of the believer. It always implicates and relates to other beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup> Glover provides the example of a patient with an illness who returns to a doctor, but the doctor says that the prescribed medicine is not working. At that point, the patient has a great deal of flexibility in choosing what beliefs to keep or reject: the patient could believe that the doctor is incompetent, that the doctor's assistants made a mistake, that the patient's own body is unique in some unexpected way, that Western medicine is ineffective, or even that Western science is entirely unable to discover truths about ailments.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Glover maintains that any person can continue to hold any belief if they would really like to<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup> (e.g., with help from <a href="/wiki/Ad_hoc_hypotheses" class="mw-redirect" title="Ad hoc hypotheses">ad hoc hypotheses</a>). One belief can be held fixed, and other beliefs will be altered around it. Glover warns that some beliefs may not be entirely <a href="/wiki/Explicit_memory" title="Explicit memory">explicitly</a> believed (e.g., some people may not realize they have racist belief systems adopted from their environment as a child). Glover believes that people tend to first realize that beliefs can change, and may be contingent on their upbringing, around age 12 or 15.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Glover emphasizes that beliefs are difficult to change. He says that one may try to rebuild one's beliefs on more secure foundations (<a href="/wiki/Axioms" class="mw-redirect" title="Axioms">axioms</a>), like building a new house, but warns that this may not be possible. Glover offers the example of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren√© Descartes">Ren√© Descartes</a>, saying about Descartes that "[h]e starts off with the characteristic beliefs of a 17th-century Frenchman; he then junks the lot, he rebuilds the system, and somehow it looks a lot like the beliefs of a 17th-century Frenchman." To Glover, belief systems are not like houses but are instead like boats. As Glover puts it: "Maybe the whole thing needs rebuilding, but inevitably at any point you have to keep enough of it intact to keep floating."<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Glover's final message is that if people talk about their beliefs, they may find more deep, relevant, philosophical ways in which they disagree (e.g., less obvious beliefs, or more deeply held beliefs). Glover thinks that people often manage to find agreements and consensus through philosophy. He says that at the very least, if people do not convert each other, they will hold their own beliefs more openmindedly and will be less likely to go to war over conflicting beliefs.<sup id="cite_ref-BitesGlover_42-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BitesGlover-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-GloverBeliefs_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GloverBeliefs-44">[44]</a></sup> the truth with open minded</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law">Law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The British philosopher <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Law" title="Stephen Law">Stephen Law</a> has described some belief systems (including belief in <a href="/wiki/Homeopathy" title="Homeopathy">homeopathy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychic_powers" class="mw-redirect" title="Psychic powers">psychic powers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alien_abduction" title="Alien abduction">alien abduction</a>) as "<a href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/claptrap" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:claptrap">claptrap</a>" and said that they "draw people in and hold them captive so they become willing slaves to victory... if you get sucked in, it can be extremely difficult to think your way clear again".<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Belief&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book">Wittgenstein, Ludwig (2007). <i>Lectures and Conversations on Aesthetics, Psychology and Religious Belief</i>. University of California Press. p.&#160;53. <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0520251814" title="Special:BookSources/0520251814">0520251814</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ABelief&amp;rft.au=Wittgenstein%2C+Ludwig&amp;rft.btitle=Lectures+and+Conversations+on+Aesthetics%2C+Psychology+and+Religious+Belief&amp;rft.date=2007&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0520251814&amp;rft.pages=53&amp;rft.pub=University+of+California+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note--35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-_35-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Miller, David Ian (2005-02-15). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sfgate.com/cgi-bin/article.cgi?file=/gate/archive/2005/02/15/findrelig.DTL">"Finding My Religion: Steve Georgiou on his faith and mentor, minimalist poet Robert Lax"</a>. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.sfgate.com/">SFGate</a><span class="reference-accessdate">. <li id="cite_note-repa-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-repa_36-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Repa, J. <li id="cite_note-larson-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-larson_37-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation journal">Larson, David B.; Susan S.
